Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner
4 min
Proper Attitude Towards a Scholar's Mistakes
Ibn Asakir defends al-Ash'ari and discusses the norm of scholarly disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Asakir argues that a scholar's mistakes do not warrant accusations of disbelief (takfir), misguidance (tadlil), or innovation (tabdi')—and how scholarly disagreement is a normal part of Islamic tradition.

Core Idea
Scholarly mistakes do not justify harsh judgments; disagreement is normal among scholars.

After mentioning that some scholars took issue with al-Ash'ari's positions, Ibn Asakir directly addresses the proper response. He asserts that these differences do not constitute disgrace, disbelief, misguidance, or innovation. He further explains that if the issues were properly investigated, they would actually lead to agreement, and he illustrates this by citing the historical example of Abu Hanifa and his two companions, who disagreed with him on many matters yet remained respected.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Asakir's main defense
Pay close attention to the phrase 'لا تكسب أبا الحسن تشنيعا ولا توجب له تكفيرا ولا تضليلا ولا تبديعا' – this is the core claim.
He lists four things that are not warranted: disgrace, takfir, tadlil, tabdi'.
2
Analyze the reasoning
Ibn Asakir argues that if one investigates the issues thoroughly, it becomes clear that the apparent disagreement is actually agreement ('الخلاف فيها حاصله الوفاق').
This suggests that deeper study resolves the tension.
3
Learn from the historical precedent
He cites the example of Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, who disagreed with him on many issues. This shows that respectful disagreement has always been part of Islamic scholarship.
The example reinforces that disagreement is not a flaw.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ibn Asakir ends with a prayer for mercy upon all scholars, emphasizing unity and respect across scholarly differences.
This frames the entire discussion in a spirit of inclusiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعتزلة فى الابتداء، ثم ان الله تعالى بين له ضلالهم فبان عما اعتقده من مذهبهم وصنف كتابا ناقضا لما صنف للمعتزلة، وقد أخد عامة أصحاب الشافعي بما استقر عليه مذهب أبي الحسن الأشعرى وصنف أصحاب الشافعي كتبا كثيرة على وفق ما ذهب إليه الأشعرى إلا أن بعض أصحابنا من أهل السنة والجماعة خطأ أبا الحسن الأشعرى في بعض المسائل مثل قوله التكوين والمكون واحد ونحوها على ما يبين فى خلال المسائل ان شاء الله تعالى فمن وقف على المسائل التي أخطأ فيها أبو الحسن وعرف خطأه فلا بأس له بالنظر في كتبه فقد امسك كتبه كثير من أصحابنا من أهل السنة والجماعة ونظروا فيها . قال الامام الحافظ رضى الله عنه وهذه المسائل التي أشار إليها لا تكسب أبا الحسن تشنيعا ولا توجب له تكفيرا ولا تضليلا ولا تبديعا، ولو حققوا الكلام فيها لحصل الاتفاق وبان بأن الخلاف فيها حاصله الوفاق، ومازال العلماء يخالف بعضهم بعضا ويقصد دفع قول خصمه إبراما ونقضا ويجتهد في إظهاره خلافه بحثا وفحصا، ولا يعتقد ذلك في حقه عيبا ونقصا وقديما ما خالف ابا حنيفة صاحباه وأجابا في كثير من المسائل بما اباه والله يتغمد جميع العلماء برحمته ويحشرنا في زمرتهم بلطفه .ورأفته
The Mu'tazila in the beginning, then Allah Almighty showed him their error, so he repented from what he believed of their madhhab and authored a book refuting what the Mu'tazila authored. And the majority of the followers of al-Shafi'i adopted what the madhhab of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari settled upon, and the followers of al-Shafi'i authored many books in accordance with what al-Ash'ari went to, except that some of our companions from Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah considered Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari mistaken in some issues, such as his statement that al-takwin (originating) and al-mukawwin (the originator) are one, and similar matters, as will be explained in the issues, Allah willing. So whoever comes across the issues in which Abu al-Hasan erred and recognizes his error, there is no harm for him in looking at his books, for many of our companions from Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah have held onto his books and looked into them. The imam al-hafiz, may Allah be pleased with him, said: These issues which he pointed out do not earn Abu al-Hasan any disgrace, nor do they necessitate his takfir (declaring disbelief), nor his tadlil (declaring misguidance), nor his tabdi' (declaring innovation). And if they had investigated the speech concerning them, agreement would have been reached and it would become clear that the difference in them is essentially agreement. And scholars have always differed with one another, intending to rebut and refute the statement of their opponent, and they strive to show him to be in error through investigation and examination, and that is not considered in his regard a flaw or deficiency. And long ago, Abu Hanifa's two companions disagreed with him and answered in many issues with what he disapproved. And may Allah envelop all scholars with His mercy and gather us in their company with His grace and kindness.

Commentary
This passage is a historical and theological defense of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari by Ibn Asakir. It begins by stating that al-Ash'ari was originally a Mu'tazili, but Allah showed him the error of their ways, so he left them and wrote a refutation. Then it notes that most Shafi'is follow al-Ash'ari's madhhab, but some Ahl al-Sunnah disagreed with him on certain issues, like the identity of takwin and mukawwin. He emphasizes that scholarly disagreement is normal and not a defect, citing the example of Abu Hanifa and his students. The passage serves to protect al-Ash'ari's reputation while acknowledging his mistakes.

التبديع

According to Ibn Asakir, what do the issues in which al-Ash'ari was mistaken not necessitate?
A) Any scholarly discussion

Key Takeaway
Scholarly mistakes do not warrant harsh accusations; respectful disagreement has always been part of Islamic scholarship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear of a scholar's mistake, what is your first inner reaction? Do you find yourself thinking less of them? What would it take for you to respond as Ibn Asakir teaches—without disgrace, takfir, tadlil, or tabdi'?
?
Think of a scholar you respect who may have made an error in 'aqeedah. Does this lesson allow you to continue learning from them? Or do you hold a hidden judgment that needs to be released? What would it mean for your heart to truly embrace that their mistake does not warrant condemnation?
?
If you were in a position of teaching or authority, how would you handle a student's theological mistake after internalizing this passage? Are you more lenient with yourself than with others? What specific step can you take this week to apply the principle of respectful disagreement in a real relationship?
